    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="53e62fecf77847c0a52b9a72b8926dfc">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b7c5c83f-9a0e-413a-b972-df8e70648a08'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="epub-sections"><div class="epub-section"><span class="epub-state">First published: </span><span class="epub-date">13 June 2024</span></div>
      <div class="epub-section"><a class="epub-doi" aria-label="Digital Object Identifier for High-Energy Earth-Abundant Cathodes with Enhanced Cationic/Anionic Redox for Sustainable and Long-Lasting Na-Ion Batteries link" href="https://doi.org/10.1002/adma.202310659">https://doi.org/10.1002/adma.202310659</a></div><div class="epub-section cited-by-count"><span>Citations: <a    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d132323708" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Layered iron/manganese-based oxides are a class of promising cathode materials for sustainable batteries due to their high energy densities and earth abundance. However, the stabilization of cationic and anionic redox reactions in these cathodes during cycling at high voltage remain elusive. Here, an electrochemically/thermally stable P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material with zero critical elements is designed for sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) to realize a highly reversible capacity of ≈210&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and good cycling stability with a capacity retention of 74% after 300 cycles at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, even when operated with a high charge cut-off voltage of 4.5&nbsp;V versus sodium metal. Combining a suite of cutting-edge characterizations and computational modeling, it is shown that Mg/Ti co-doping leads to stabilized surface/bulk structure at high voltage and high temperature, and more importantly, enhances cationic/anionic redox reaction reversibility over extended cycles with the suppression of other undesired oxygen activities. This work fundamentally deepens the failure mechanism of Fe/Mn-based layered cathodes and highlights the importance of dopant engineering to achieve high-energy and earth-abundant cathode material for sustainable and long-lasting NIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310659-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310659-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Large-scale electrochemical-energy-storage systems have the potential to transform the electric grid.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0001_R_d132323700e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have played a dominant role in portable electronic devices and electric vehicles, the uneven distribution and limited abundance of lithium resources raise serious concerns on the sustainability and total project cost (200–500&nbsp;USD&nbsp;kWh<sup>−1</sup>) of LIBs for grid-scale energy storage application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0002_R_d132323700e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have been considered as a better candidate due to the high abundance of Na resources and similar working principle to that of LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0003_R_d132323700e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, commercial NIBs have been developed with energy densities comparable with the commercial LiFePO<sub>4</sub>-based LIBs. According to Argonne BatPaC simulation, the increase of cathode capacity could greatly lift the cell-specific energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0004_R_d132323700e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Energy Lett.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, high capacity (≈200&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) has been hardly attained in NIB cathodes under stringent electrochemical/thermo stability requirements. On the other hand, to really enable low-cost and sustainable NIBs, it needs to completely avoid the use of scarce and expensive Co, Ni, and Li that raise serious concerns on the supply chain of raw materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0005_R_d132323700e677" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>-<a href="#adma202310659-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0008_R_d132323700e677" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Energy Lett.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Indeed, despite stable cycle life, many reported sodium cathode materials still contain these elements, which fail to fully fulfil the expected low cost and sustainability of NIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0009_R_d132323700e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Mater.">9</a>-<a href="#adma202310659-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0012_R_d132323700e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Sustainability">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To increase the capacity of cathode materials, oxygen anionic redox reactions (ARRs) have been introduced in Li/Na-based layered oxides to provide space for charge compensation beyond the conventional cationic redox reactions (CRRs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0013_R_d132323700e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Joule">13</a>-<a href="#adma202310659-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0015_R_d132323700e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the activation of lattice O<sup>2−</sup> ions at high voltages often leads to the irreversible release of oxygen, accelerating the structural reconstruction and resulting in the rapid decay of both capacity and voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0016_R_d132323700e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Matter">16</a>-<a href="#adma202310659-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0018_R_d132323700e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Chem.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the harness of oxygen's electrochemistry to realize reversible ARR is critical for achieving high-energy cathode materials, which remains challenging and requires innovative structural design. Compared with Li-ion systems, especially with the Li-rich configuration that seems to be highly irreversible on oxygen behaviors,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0019_R_d132323700e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Joule">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> various Na-ion systems have displayed reversible ARR but during the initial few cycles only.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0011_R_d132323700e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nature">11</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0013_R_d132323700e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Joule">13</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0014_R_d132323700e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0016_R_d132323700e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Matter">16</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0019_R_d132323700e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Joule">19</a>-<a href="#adma202310659-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0035_R_d132323700e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nat. Energy">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These works indicate the promising potential of Na-ion electrodes based on ARR, which inspires us to explore optimization strategies that could enable high-voltage operation of Na-ion cathode materials through ARR while maintaining their structural stability over extended cycles.</p>
         
         <p>Mn and Fe are two highly abundant elements in the earth's crust, and thus highly preferable for designing cathode materials for NIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0036_R_d132323700e715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, rapid capacity degradation and severe structural transformation can easily take place in Fe/Mn-based cathode materials at high voltages due to 1) uncontrollable reaction pathways of oxygen ions, 2) detrimental structural degradations associated with Jahn–Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> during charge and discharge, 3) the distortion of FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedrons and spontaneous migration/trap of Fe<sup>3+</sup> into alkali metal layer particularly during cycling at high voltage (&gt;4.0&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0035_R_d132323700e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nat. Energy">35</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0037_R_d132323700e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Mater.">37</a>-<a href="#adma202310659-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0040_R_d132323700e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 4) complicated phase transitions with the slide of TM layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0041_R_d132323700e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Science">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the operational safety of batteries becomes also critical for grid-scale energy storage. Thus, the improvement of thermal stability of high-voltage layered oxide cathode materials through rational structure strategies is urgent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0042_R_d132323700e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Chem. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, despite intensive efforts of element substitution,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0032_R_d132323700e745" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> surface coating<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0043_R_d132323700e750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano Energy">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and electrolyte optimization,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0044_R_d132323700e756" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Energy Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to the best of our knowledge, all the merits of a reversible capacity of &gt;200&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, long cycle life, good thermal stability, and minimum voltage fading during high-voltage operation have not been achieved yet.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we report a low-cost and environmentally friendly P2-type, Fe/Mn-based material with Mg/Ti co-doping (Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, NFMMT) for NIBs. This material delivers a high reversible capacity of over 200&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and good cycling performance at high specific currents (74% capacity retention at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles, 73.1% capacity retention at 400&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles) within a voltage range of 1.5–4.5&nbsp;V. To the best of our knowledge, this cycle performance is better than that of most reported sodium layered Fe/Mn-based oxide cathodes with such a high charge cut-off voltage of 4.5&nbsp;V. The large reversible capacity originates from the combination of transition metal CRR and oxygen ARR, which is clarified by a series of state-of-the-art techniques. Particularly, through a comparative study with the undoped Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM), we show that Mg/Ti co-doping enhances both cationic and anionic redox while, surprisingly, maintaining excellent cycling stability even upon high voltage operations.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310659-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310659-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310659-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structures of NFM and NFMMT</h3>
            
            <p>Both NFM and NFMMT were synthesized by the traditional solid-state reaction. The inductively coupled plasma (ICP) spectroscopy results suggest a composition of Na<sub>0.668</sub>Fe<sub>0.398</sub>Mn<sub>0.603</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.668</sub>Fe<sub>0.295</sub>Mn<sub>0.501</sub>Mg<sub>0.099</sub>Ti<sub>0.099</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for NFM and NFMMT, respectively. The structures of these two materials were investigated by synchrotron X-ray diffraction (XRD). As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0001">1a</a> (NFMMT) and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) (NFM), both materials exhibit a typical P2-type structure (space group P63/mmc (194)). In this structure, the “ABAB” stacking sequence of oxygen packing constitutes the main frame, TM ions are located in the center of TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedrons, and Na<sup>+</sup> ions occupy the prismatic sites between TM layers. The Rietveld refinement results (Tables&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1,S2</a>, Supporting Information) further indicate both materials has no&nbsp;impurities. The refined lattice parameters of NFM and NFMMT are <i>a&nbsp;</i>=&nbsp;2.922(3)&nbsp;Å, <i>c&nbsp;</i>=&nbsp;11.242(5)&nbsp;Å (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) and <i>a&nbsp;</i>=&nbsp;2.917(2)&nbsp;Å, <i>c&nbsp;</i>=&nbsp;11.239(8)&nbsp;Å (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), respectively. The differences in lattice parameters <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> are smaller than the refinement errors, indicating the nearly unchanged lattice structures after Mg/Ti doping. A structural illustration of NFMMT is provided in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0001">1b</a>, showing the “ABBA” stacking mode and two possible Na<sup>+</sup> occupation sites, including Na<sub>e</sub> (sharing edges with six TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedrons) and Na<sub>f</sub> (sharing faces with the upper and lower TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedrons). Besides, both NFM and NFMMT can maintain the layered structures even after exposure to air for one month, as indicated by the XRD results in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2,S3</a> (Supporting Information), which are important for practical applications. Nevertheless, slight peak broadening were observed for NFM instead of NFMMT, indicating the slightly enhanced air stability by Mg/Ti doping.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310659-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/291147dd-2c19-4a35-9277-be8b969a4a3f/adma202310659-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/291147dd-2c19-4a35-9277-be8b969a4a3f/adma202310659-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/64380e11-6461-4023-a316-23e6946771b4/adma202310659-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/291147dd-2c19-4a35-9277-be8b969a4a3f/adma202310659-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310659-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310659" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterizations of NFMMT. a) Synchrotron XRD patterns with structural refinements. b) Structural schematic image with the Na<sub>e</sub> and Na<sub>f</sub> occupation sites. c) HAADF-STEM image. d) ABF-STEM image. e,f) High-resolution ABF-STEM images of NFMMT showing the Na<sub>e</sub> (e) and Na<sub>f</sub> (f) occupation sites in the marked regions in (d).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The morphology of NFMMT was examined by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), showing the typical particle size of 1–2&nbsp;µm, a bit smaller that of NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). For both materials, all the relevant elements are homogeneously distributed within the particles, as shown by the element maps in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6,S7</a> (Supporting Information). High-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) and annular bright-field (ABF) scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) were further employed to investigate the structures of NFMMT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0001">1c–f</a>) and NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). For NFMMT, TM ions appear as bright dots in the HAADF image along a straight line, and light element Na and O ions appear as faint dark dots in the ABF image, showing clear “ABBA” stacking sequence in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0001">1e,f</a>. The two Na<sup>+</sup> location sites (Na<sub>e</sub> and Na<sub>f</sub>) can also be clearly identified in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0001">1e,f</a>, respectively. Similar structures can be identified for NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). Combined with fast Fourier transformation patterns, the STEM results confirm the P2 structure for both NFM and NFMMT, in good agreement with the synchrotron XRD results. In addition, the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) Mg-2p peaks at ≈49.3&nbsp;eV and the Ti-2p peaks at 457 and 464&nbsp;eV suggest the valence states of Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> at the surface of NFMMT (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information respectively).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310659-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performances of NFM and NFMMT were investigated in half cells with sodium metal as the anode. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a> (Supporting Information) shows the charge and discharge profiles of NFM, indicating an initial discharge-specific capacity of 186&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> within a voltage range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V. By further increasing the charge cut-off voltage from 4.3 to 4.5&nbsp;V, the discharge capacity of NFM increases to 216&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with an average operating voltage of 2.6&nbsp;V (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0002">2a</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The increased capacity originates from both the CRR of the Fe element and the ARR of the O element, which will be discussed in the following part.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310659-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/266761a4-0090-46cf-98f2-cf063e8c5c64/adma202310659-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/266761a4-0090-46cf-98f2-cf063e8c5c64/adma202310659-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fc3fdb43-15cd-446d-b333-3b3b00d34efc/adma202310659-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/266761a4-0090-46cf-98f2-cf063e8c5c64/adma202310659-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310659-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310659" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of NFM, NFMM, NFMT, and NFMMT within 1.5–4.5&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). a,b) Charge and discharge profiles of NFM (a) and NFMMT (b) at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. c,d) Discharge profiles of NFM (c) and NFMMT (d) showing the voltage decay features. e) Discharge capacities of NFM, NFMM, NFMT, and NFMMT cathode during cycling at a specific current of 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after formation at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for 3 cycles. f) Cycling performance of NFM and NFMMT cathode during charge/discharge at a specific current of 400&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Hollow and solid symbols represent charge and discharge capacities, respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a> (Supporting Information) shows that NFMMT delivers an initial discharge capacity of 177&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> when charged to 4.3&nbsp;V. When the charge cut-off voltage was increased from 4.3 to 4.5&nbsp;V, the initial discharge capacity of NFMMT was increased to 209&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0002">2b</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover,&nbsp;NFMMT exhibits a longer voltage slope than that of NFM at the high voltage range of 3.6–4.5&nbsp;V, corresponding to the mixture of both CRR and ARR. Typically, voltage decay is often seen in layered oxides owing to the irreversible structural transformation during the cycling process, which will decrease the specific energy of batteries. Although the doped inactive Mg and Ti slightly reduce the capacity of NFMMT, the discharge voltage can be better maintained in NFMMT than that in NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0002">2c,d</a>), which is beneficial&nbsp;to maintain the specific energy of the battery.</p>
            
            <p>The capacity retention of NFMMT during cycling at high specific currents is significantly improved when compared with that of NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0002">2a,b</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0002">2e</a>, the reversible capacity of NFM degrades rapidly upon cycling, leading to a capacity retention of only 27.9% (the capacity retention was normalized to the first discharge capacity at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the same below) after 150 cycles at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. NFM with Ti doping or Mg doping only were also synthesized for comparison (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0002">2e</a>, a single dopant cannot effectively suppress the capacity fading during high-voltage cycling, indicating the necessity of co-doping. Indeed, NFMMT with Mg/Ti co-doping exhibits a much better capacity retention of 74% after 300 cycles during charge/discharge at 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> within 1.5–4.5&nbsp;V. Even at a high specific current of 400&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, NFMMT could still deliver a high initial capacity of 167.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 73.1% after 200 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0002">2f</a>). Moreover, as shown in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), NFMMT delivers both higher capacity and better cycle stability, superior to most reported sodium-layered Fe/Mn-based cathodes with a high cut-off voltage of &gt;4.0&nbsp;V. In addition, NFMMT exhibits a higher capacity retention in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte than that in a common NP30 electrolyte (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC: DMC [1:1 v/v], Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), which has been ascribed to the synergistic effect between ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> and O<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> anions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0021_R_d132323700e1086" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Joule">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, after being exposed in air for 15&nbsp;days, NFMMT still shows much better electrochemical performance than NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information), further indicating the enhanced air stability by Mg/Ti co-doping. The fabrication of full cells using anodes such as hard carbon is important for the practical application of the developed cathodes. However, P2-type cathodes suffer from inherent sodium deficiency, which requires pre-sodiation for full cells, by strategies such as the electrochemical method, self-sacrificial route, and direct sodium compensation process, which will be further studied at the pouch-cell level.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0045_R_d132323700e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Small">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310659-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Structure Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>Synchrotron in situ XRD was employed to monitor the structural evolution of the two materials during insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. The in situ XRD patterns of NFM in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information) show that the P2 (002) peak gradually shifts to lower angles before being charged to 3.6&nbsp;V, suggesting the expansion of lattice parameter <i>c</i>. Meanwhile, the (010), (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4764d0b7-7bae-49b5-89cf-332586d41323/adma202310659-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202310659:adma202310659-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202310659-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>1</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{1}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>20) and (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d47d408d-3cd0-4cfc-8e89-afb757b73a0f/adma202310659-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202310659:adma202310659-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202310659-math-0002.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>1</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{1}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>22) peaks gradually shift to higher angles, suggesting the shrinkage of lattice parameter <i>a</i>. The intensities of all peaks become lower&nbsp;(voltage&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;4.1&nbsp;V) and then higher (voltage&nbsp;&gt;&nbsp;4.1&nbsp;V), indicating the phase transition process of NFM. The (002) peak shifts positively to 1.32° at the end of the charge process, demonstrating the formation of the O2 phase,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0046_R_d132323700e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem. Mater.">46</a>-<a href="#adma202310659-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0048_R_d132323700e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Electrochem. Commun.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is related to the slide of TM layers and results in the conversion of Na<sup>+</sup> occupation from prismatic to octahedrons sites. An intermediate OP4 phase during the phase transition could be identified,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0049_R_d132323700e1148" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">49</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0050_R_d132323700e1148" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Power Sources">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with an alternate arrangement of prismatic (P2 phase) and octahedron (O2 phase) Na occupation sites, which is related to the shrinkage of lattice parameter <i>c</i> and weakening of the XRD peaks. Upon the discharge process to 3.2&nbsp;V, the (002) peak shifts to lower angles and becomes weaker, owing to the expansion of lattice parameter <i>c</i> and phase transition from O2 to P2 through the OP4 phase. When further discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, the (002) peak shifts to higher angles, demonstrating the shrinkage of lattice parameter <i>c</i> in the P2 phase. The (010), (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a0f8b2cc-199b-4547-811a-740f21848137/adma202310659-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202310659:adma202310659-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202310659-math-0003.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>1</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{1}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>20), and (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f3fff648-af98-4956-9fc5-62447418e516/adma202310659-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202310659:adma202310659-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202310659-math-0004.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>1</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{1}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>22) peaks gradually shift back to lower angles, corresponding to the expansion of lattice parameter <i>a</i>. Note that new small peaks appear at ≈1.24° and 2.45°, corresponding to the formation of the P’2 phase. Therefore, NFM experiences the complicated P2-OP4-O2-OP4-P2-P’2 phase transition with large volume change during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310659-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e0373e31-4a2a-46e1-848b-892623decb46/adma202310659-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e0373e31-4a2a-46e1-848b-892623decb46/adma202310659-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d8072dd6-746d-4e19-9f1f-87a5d8c0eedd/adma202310659-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e0373e31-4a2a-46e1-848b-892623decb46/adma202310659-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310659-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310659" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical and thermal stabilities of NFM and NFMMT. a,b) Synchrotron in situ XRD patterns of NFM (a) and NFMMT (b) in the initial cycle at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with corresponding initial charge/discharge profiles (right). c,d) The 2D contour plot of HEXRD patterns of (002) peak of NFM (c) and NFMMT (d) as a function of temperature.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>As for NFMMT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), all the XRD peaks exhibit a similar shifting tendency to that of NFM. However, there is no intensity recovery of the weakened XRD peaks, and the (002) peak only shifts to 1.28° at the end of the charge process, demonstrating the P2-to-OP4 phase transition during the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. Note that there is no obvious new peak evolution during the charge/discharge process, suggesting a simple P2-OP4-P2 phase transition of NFMMT. As demonstrated in the literature, doping trace amount of Ti will interrupt the in-plane Na-vacancy ordering, weaken the Jahn–Teller effect, minimize the relative dissolution of Mn ions, and thus enhancing the structure stability during the charge/discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0051_R_d132323700e1213" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Small Methods">51</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0052_R_d132323700e1213" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Energy Environ. Sci.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, Mg ions in transition-metal oxides will greatly stabilize oxygen by forming strong Mg─O bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0019_R_d132323700e1219" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Joule">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These effects explain the result that NFM experiences the complicated P2-OP4-O2-OP4-P2-P’2 phase transition, while the Mg/Ti co-doped NFMMT experiences the simple P2-OP4-P2 phase transition.</p>
            
            <p>At the end of the discharge process, peak broadening is observed in both NFM and NFMMT, which may be ascribed to the multiple-phase transition and changes of the lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0053_R_d132323700e1227" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Scr. Mater.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The variations of lattice parameters and cell volume are further calculated by fitting the in situ XRD results. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), the lattice parameters of NFM experience a series of changes during multiple phase transitions. The volume change is calculated to be 8.21% (based on the equation: <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1cb08468-206f-4901-82b2-4e4b93b28040/adma202310659-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202310659:adma202310659-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202310659-math-0005.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>volume</mi>

                           <mi>change</mi>

                        </mrow>
                        <mo>=</mo>
                        <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>

                           <mrow>
