                                    <mi>x</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msub>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mo>−</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>V</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>m</mi>
                                    <mi>i</mi>
                                    <mi>n</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msub>
                           </mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>V</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>p</mi>
                                 <mi>r</mi>
                                 <mi>i</mi>
                                 <mi>s</mi>
                                 <mi>t</mi>
                                 <mi>i</mi>
                                 <mi>n</mi>
                                 <mi>e</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </msub>
                        </mfrac>
                        <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        <mo>×</mo>
                        <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        <mn>100</mn>
                        <mo>%</mo>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{volume\ change\ \ }} = \ \frac{{{{V}_{max}}\ - \ {{V}_{min}}}}{{{{V}_{pristine}}}}\ \times \ 100\% $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>) during the first cycle. For NFMMT, the simple P2-OP4-P2 phase transition is reflected from the lattice parameter changes (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information) during the first cycle, yielding a calculated cell volume change of 3.9%, which is much smaller than that of NFM.</p>
            
            <p>Synchrotron ex situ XRD measurements of&nbsp;NFM and NFMMT at pristine state, first, second, and 50th cycles were further conducted. All the peaks in the pristine NFM are indexed to the P2 structure (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). After the first charge process, the (002) peak shifts to a higher angle with the reduction of intensity, indicating the formation of the O2 structure. At the first discharged state, NFM exhibits a composite structure characteristic. The (002) peak in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrates the generation of the P’2 structure, which agrees well with the in situ XRD results. Moreover, NFM in the charged states of the second and 50th cycles exhibit a P2/O2 composite structure. At the discharge state, the content of the P’2 structure gradually increased (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a>, Supporting Information). By contrast, all the structures of NFMMT at the charged states of the first, second, and 50th cycles belong to the OP4 structure without any new peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). The discharged states of the first, second, and 50th cycles exhibit a single peak that is similar to the P2 structure but with slight lattice parameter variation. The results further illustrate the better structural stability of NFMMT during the extended charge/discharge process.</p>
            
            <p>In layered oxides, when Mn ions migrate from Mn site (0, 0, 0) to Na<sub>e</sub> sites (1/3, 2/3, 3/4), the relative intensities of (012) and (013) peaks become stronger than that of the (002) peak. On the other hand, upon the migration of Mn ions from the Mn site to Na<sub>f</sub> sites (0, 0, 1/4), the relative intensities of (004), (100), and (015) peaks become stronger.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0054_R_d132323700e1308" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Nat. Commun.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, peak fitting is further employed to analyze the TM migrations during the charge and discharge process. As shown in Tables&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), all peaks become broader with the increase of cycle numbers, which may be ascribed to the multiple phase transition and large changes of lattice parameters. The peak intensity ratios (004)/(002) and (013)/(002) become notably enlarged for both materials after the first cycle, suggesting the migration of Mn ions to the Na layer. After 50 cycles, the ratio of peaks (004) and (002) becomes decreased in NFM, which may be due to the dissolution of Mn ions. It is worth noting that the rise of relative intensities of peaks (004) and (013) is smaller in NFMMT than that in NFM, suggesting the Mn migration from the TM layer to the Na layer may be suppressed by the co-doping of Mg and Ti. Moreover, the ICP test suggests that the Mn dissolution of NFMMT (218&nbsp;ppm) is greatly reduced compared with that of NFM (1308&nbsp;ppm) during cycling, which is beneficial to the performance of NFMMT.</p>
            
            <p>SEM was also conducted for both NFM and NFMMT after 100 cycles. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information), no crack is observed for both materials at the pristine state. After 100 cycles, small cracks are observed in NFM while no obvious crack could be observed in NFMMT. The formation of cracks of NFM after 100 cycles may be due to the large volume change during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion process. TEM studies further show that NFM can easily form surface distortion and cracking, while NFMMT shows well-maintained layered structures and less cracking at the surface, as shown in&nbsp;Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information). The results further confirm the structural stability of NFMMT during cycling.</p>
            
            <p>The thermal stability of cathode materials strongly influences the safety of batteries. To study the thermal performance of the two materials, high-temperature in situ XRD was further employed to examine the structural stability of charged NFM and NFMMT cathodes at elevated temperatures. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0003">3c</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information) present the in situ XRD patterns of 4.5&nbsp;V-charged NFM during heating from 30 to 400&nbsp;°C, while Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information) present those of NFMMT. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0003">3c</a>, the (002) peak located at 1.3° belongs to the layered O2 structure. With the increase of temperature, the (002) peak gradually shifts to lower angles due to the lattice expansion at elevated temperature. When further heating the charged NFM to ≈175&nbsp;°C, a small peak at a lower angle corresponding to the rock salt structure appears, while the layered (002) peak gradually becomes weaker. The layered (002) peak disappears at ≈320&nbsp;°C, suggesting the O2-to-rock salt phase transition. The results can also be observed clearly in the enlarged XRD result between 1° to 1.5° (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0003">3c</a>). The correlation coefficient matrix exhibits the relationship of adjacent XRD patterns. Thus, the 2D color-filled correlation coefficient matrix image (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25a</a>, Supporting Information) for NFM is derived from XRD results, further confirming the phase transition between 175 and 320&nbsp;°C. For NFMMT, the peak located at ≈1.2° belongs to the layered OP4 structure, which exhibits a similar shift to lower angles with the temperature increase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0003">3d</a>). Compared with that of the charged NFM cathode, the layered (002) peak of the charged NFMMT cathode becomes weak at a higher temperature of 290&nbsp;°C. More importantly, no new peak appears during the entire heating process. Enlarged XRD result (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information) and 2D color-filled correlation coefficient matrix image (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25b</a>, Supporting Information) further confirm that the charged NFMMT is quite stable even up to 400&nbsp;°C.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310659-sec-0060-title">2.4 Metal CRR Activity and Stability</h3>
            
            <p>The charge compensation mechanism of NFMMT was studied by synchrotron X-ray absorption spectroscopy. The X-ray absorption near-edge spectroscopy (XANES) K-edge spectra of Mn in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> (Supporting Information) show two small peaks located at 6539 and 6542&nbsp;eV, indicating the MnO<sub>6</sub> coordination. The enlarged Mn K-edge XANES spectra during the charge process is presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0004">4a</a>. In the pristine state, the valence of Mn is +3.99 by comparing with the reference Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and MnO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0050_R_d132323700e1392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Power Sources">50</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0055_R_d132323700e1392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Energy Lett.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the charge process, the edges do not exhibit visible shifts, with only slight lineshape changes, suggesting the negligible change of the local environment of Mn<sup>4+</sup> ions. During the discharge process from 4.5 to 2.5&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0004">4b</a>), no obvious edge shift can be observed either, suggesting the maintenance of the Mn<sup>4+</sup> state in this range. When further discharged to 1.5&nbsp;V, the Mn K-edge XANES spectrum shifts to the lower energy region, corresponding to the reduction of Mn<sup>4+</sup>. Extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra of Mn were further examined as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0004">4c</a>. Two peaks are observed at ≈1.4 and 2.5&nbsp;Å, corresponding to the Mn─O and Mn─TM bonds, respectively. During the process of charging to 4.5&nbsp;V and then discharging to 2.5&nbsp;V, the Mn─O bond length in NFMMT maintains constant to be 1.4&nbsp;Å with no shift. Nevertheless, further discharging to 1.5&nbsp;V results in the obvious shift of the Mn─O bond to 1.52&nbsp;Å, demonstrating the reduction of Mn<sup>4+</sup> only at low voltages below 2.5&nbsp;V.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310659-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/180de875-6f8f-40ee-8d54-9d4de15421e8/adma202310659-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/180de875-6f8f-40ee-8d54-9d4de15421e8/adma202310659-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a2101783-b80f-4d7a-b511-3790eea9cc8b/adma202310659-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/180de875-6f8f-40ee-8d54-9d4de15421e8/adma202310659-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310659-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310659" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XAS and <sup>57</sup>Fe Mössbauer characterizations of NFMMT. a,b) Enlarged XAS Mn K-edge spectra during the initial charge (a) and discharge (b) processes. c) The corresponding Fourier transform results. d, e) Enlarged XAS Fe K-edge spectra during the initial charge (d) and discharge (e) processes. f) The corresponding Fourier transform results. g–i) <sup>57</sup>Fe Mössbauer spectra of NFMMT at pristine (g), fully charged (h), and fully discharged (i) states. D stands for discharge, while C stands for charge, the same below.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Compared with the relatively inactive Mn, Fe has an active redox reaction across a wide energy range, as demonstrated by the K-edge XANES spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). The valence state of Fe is determined to be ≈+2.98 in the pristine NFMMT by comparing with the reference Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0056_R_d132323700e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Coord. Chem. Rev.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Throughout the charge process, the Fe K-edge spectra continuously shift to the higher energy region, corresponding to the oxidation of Fe during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0004">4d</a>). During the discharge process above 2.5&nbsp;V, the edge gradually shifts to the lower energy region, indicating the reduction of Fe during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0004">4e</a>). Pre-edge peaks at ≈7112&nbsp;eV in Fe K-edge profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> (Supporting Information) are slightly intensified during the charge process, which is attributed to an electric quadrupole transition of a Fe 1s electron to the Fe 3d orbitals and an electric dipole transition of a Fe 1s electron to the hybridized O 2p-Fe 3d orbitals. The broadness and high intensities of the pre-edge peaks indicate the large contribution of the latter transition and the hole formation in the hybridized O 2p orbitals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0057_R_d132323700e1470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Small">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Note that there is no obvious edge shift except a slight shape change when further discharged from 2.5 to 1.5&nbsp;V, suggesting no further reduction of Fe takes place in this low-voltage range. As shown in the EXAFS result (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0004">4f</a>), the Fe─O bond length gradually increases from 1.51&nbsp;Å upon the charge and decreases back upon discharge to 2.5&nbsp;V. Peaks located at ≈1.1&nbsp;Å are ascribed to the sidelobe effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0058_R_d132323700e1478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Energy Environ. Sci.">58</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0059_R_d132323700e1478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is clear that Fe is active in the high voltage range. Similar situations are also observed for Mn and Fe K-edge profiles in the second and even 50th cycles (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29,S30</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Assessment of XANES over cycling is also derived from the synchrotron ex situ XAS results in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a> (Supporting Information). According to the change of valence states of Mn, it is calculated that the Mn-induced capacity can retain 89.8% in the second cycle and 64.9% in the 50th cycle. The continuous loss of Mn-induced capacity may be attributed to the Jahn–Teller effect and Mn dissolution upon cycling. Similarly, it is calculated that the Fe-induced capacity can retain 67.2% in the second cycle and 65.8% in the 50th cycle. Obviously, the loss of Fe-induced capacity mainly occurs during the first cycle, which may be related with the migration of Fe ions in the first cycle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0037_R_d132323700e1496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Mater.">37</a>-<a href="#adma202310659-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0039_R_d132323700e1496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Environ. Sci.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The migration of TM might trigger the activation of lattice oxygen in layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0060_R_d132323700e1503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">60</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0061_R_d132323700e1503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Adv. Energy Mater.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fe migration is also recognized as a degradation mechanism for Fe-rich Li and Na compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0035_R_d132323700e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nat. Energy">35</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0040_R_d132323700e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0061_R_d132323700e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Adv. Energy Mater.">61</a>-<a href="#adma202310659-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0063_R_d132323700e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ex situ <sup>57</sup>Fe Mössbauer spectra on NFMMT at pristine, fully charged (4.5&nbsp;V), and fully discharged (1.5&nbsp;V) states in the first cycle were further acquired, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0004">4g–i</a>. The isomer shift (IS), quadrupole splitting, and relative concentrations of Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> ions are obtained by peak fitting (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0060_R_d132323700e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">60</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0061_R_d132323700e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Adv. Energy Mater.">61</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0064_R_d132323700e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Chem. Mater.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0004">4g</a> shows that the spectrum of pristine NFMMT is composed of a single sharp doublet, in good agreement with that of Fe<sup>3+</sup> in the FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedral environment (Fe<sup>3+</sup>(O)). At the fully charged state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0004">4h</a>), the spectrum becomes asymmetric in the doublet, which could be fitted into three components including Fe<sup>3+</sup>(O), Fe<sup>3+</sup>(T) (Fe<sup>3+</sup> in the FeO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedral environment), and Fe<sup>4+</sup>O<sub>6</sub>. The area percentages of Fe<sup>3+</sup>(O), Fe<sup>3+</sup>(T), and Fe<sup>4+</sup>O<sub>6</sub> are 55.0%, 10.1%, and 34.9%, respectively. The formation of Fe<sup>4+</sup>O<sub>6</sub> demonstrates the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> redox of NFMMT at high voltage, in agreement with the XAS Fe K-edge spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0004">4d–f</a>. The formation of Fe<sup>3+</sup>(T) indicates that a small portion of Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions could migrate during the charge process. At the fully discharge state, Fe in NFMMT returns to Fe<sup>3+</sup>(O) and Fe<sup>3+</sup>(T), with area percentages of 94.7% and 5.3%, respectively, suggesting the Fe migration in the first cycle is partially reversible. The above results indicate that Fe<sup>3+</sup> in NFMMT would undergo oxidation to Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Fe migration upon charge. The Fe migration process may favor the formation of unpaired electrons in the O 2p orbital, which triggers the oxygen anionic redox reaction for NFMMT.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310659-sec-0070-title">2.5 Oxygen ARR Activity and Stability</h3>
            
            <p>Considering the molar ratio of Fe (0.3) in the NFMMT formula, Fe can contribute a theoretical specific capacity of ≈80&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> based on its redox reaction. Therefore, ARR becomes necessary to achieve the tested high capacity (≈200&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). It thus becomes important to characterize the ARR activities, especially at high voltages. As clarified recently, the conventional O K-edge absorption spectroscopy is interfered by the transition metal characters, and its variation is misleading in studying ARR states.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0043_R_d132323700e1605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano Energy">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We therefore employed the high-efficiency O K-edge mapping of resonant inelastic X-ray scattering (mRIXS), which has been established as a powerful probe to scrutinize the lattice ARR activities in various cathodes of NIBs/LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0013_R_d132323700e1610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Joule">13</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0019_R_d132323700e1610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Joule">19</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0020_R_d132323700e1610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It has been found that a dedicated mRIXS feature at ≈531&nbsp;eV excitation and 523.7&nbsp;eV emission energies can be used to fingerprint the lattice ARR, and its intensity variation could be tracked to monitor the reversibility and decay of the reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0019_R_d132323700e1616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Joule">19</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0020_R_d132323700e1616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0005">5</a> presents the initial charge/discharge process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0005">5a,c</a>) and the continuous evolutions of the mRIXS maps (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0005">5b,d</a>) for both materials, in which the intensities around the characteristic energy range of ARR get enhanced at high voltages (red circles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0005">5b,d</a>). This could also be directly seen from the RIXS cut profiles at 531&nbsp;eV excitation energy (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31c,f</a>, Supporting Information), which show clearly enhanced intensities ≈523.7&nbsp;eV at high voltages compared with the pristine/discharged NFM or NFMMT. Upon discharge, the signals of the lattice oxidized oxygen in both materials disappear, indicating a reversible ARR for both. When the system is discharged to 1.5&nbsp;V, the typical TM─O hybridization feature ≈525&nbsp;eV emission energy has been significantly enhanced, indicating the signals are not from the layered oxide but from a surface layer, which has been often found in Li-rich compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0049_R_d132323700e1639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At this time, details on cathode–electrolyte-interphase remain elusive and a grand challenge topic in the battery field, which is not a topic of this work, but we note that the patterns observed here are close to surface peroxide species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0065_R_d132323700e1645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although this takes place only at the deeply discharged 1.5&nbsp;V state, the surface reaction typically affects the cycling stability, which also indicates that future improvement of the material through surface coating may be possible. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a> (Supporting Information) presents a comparative study of the ARR activities in NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31a,b</a>, Supporting Information) and NFMMT (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31d,e</a>, Supporting Information) at representative electrochemical states. It can be clearly seen that the charge of initial NFM or NFMMT to 4.5&nbsp;V led to the change of the mRIXS maps and the appearance of the ARR features, as indicated by the red arrows and red circles. It is important to emphasize that mRIXS results reveal that the ARR is much stronger in NFMMT compared with NFM, which directly suggests that ARR gets enhanced in the Mg/Ti co-doped system. The Mg dopant is highly ionic and generates an O environment similar to that in the alkali-rich configuration, which could promote the activation of ARR.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0020_R_d132323700e1659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0023_R_d132323700e1659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Chem.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The above mRIXS result shows that NFMMT has a more noticeable ARR than NFM, implying the important role of Mg dopant on the activation of lattice oxygen. On the other hand, as suggested by the above ex situ <sup>57</sup>Fe Mössbauer result, the Fe migration may also promote the formation of unpaired electrons to trigger the oxygen ARR.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310659-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fcd9209c-100a-4d68-9dd1-69a55afee074/adma202310659-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fcd9209c-100a-4d68-9dd1-69a55afee074/adma202310659-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4fe71d5a-d5ca-4973-849b-272065882962/adma202310659-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fcd9209c-100a-4d68-9dd1-69a55afee074/adma202310659-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310659-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310659" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">mRIXS studies of NFM and NFMMT. a,b) Initial Charge and discharge profiles (a) and corresponding mRIXS image of O K-edge (b) of NFM. c,d) Initial Charge and discharge profiles (c) and corresponding mRIXS image of O K-edge (d) of NFMMT.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) measurements for both NFM and NFMMT in the initial three cycles were conducted to analyze the gas releases. As shown in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32,S33</a> (Supporting Information), both NFM and NFMMT release CO<sub>2</sub> during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction process, which may come from the decomposition of carbonates or the reactions between oxidative species and electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0030_R_d132323700e1696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Energy Lett.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In general, although no obvious released O<sub>2</sub> is observed for both NFM and NFMMT during the whole cycle process, it can be clearly seen that NFMMT exhibits a much smaller CO<sub>2</sub> release, indicating better interfacial stability and better oxygen redox stability by Mg/Ti co-doping.</p>
            
            <p>Theoretical calculations were also conducted for both NFM and NFMMT to understand the mechanism of enhanced ARR in NFMMT (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0006">6</a>). The models of NFM and NFMMT for the first principles calculations are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0006">6a,d</a>, respectively. The calculated results of the density of state are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0006">6b,e</a>, respectively. Obviously, enhanced oxygen redox activity can be observed in NFMMT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0006">6f</a>) when compared with NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0006">6c</a>). The quantitative analysis of the density of the state of NFM and NFMMT was further proceeded. The proportions of O 2p and TM 3d electrons near the Fermi energy level in the energy range of −1–0&nbsp;eV for NFM and NFMMT were further quantified. The proportions of O 2p and TM 3d of NFM are 71% and 29%, while the proportions of O 2p and TM 3d of NFM are 84% and 16%, respectively. To further understand the effect of Mg and Ti on the charge states of O, the partial density of the state with different O coordination configurations has been further plotted (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0006">6g</a>). Compared to those in other configurations, the O 2p electrons in O1-Mg2Mn1 coordinated with two Mg and one Mn are obviously clustered near the Fermi energy level and have higher reactivity. This could be ascribed to that the hybridization between Mg and O is weak and the weakly hybridized O 2p electrons due to the Na─O─Mg configuration are reactive during the charge process. In addition, a comparison of the highest occupied state (HOS) charge density plots in the −1–0&nbsp;eV energy range reveals that most of the high-energy O 2p electrons are clustered around O1-Mg2Mn1 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0006">6h</a>). When half of Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted, NFMMT produces distinct unoccupied states of O above the Fermi energy level, while NFM is dominated by TM transitions (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the Bader charge changes of TM and O in NFM are 0.086 and 0.108, while those of TM and O in NFMMT are 0.065 and 0.121, respectively. In addition, the HOS charge density results of the desodiation state indicate that the high-energy electrons of NFM are clustered around Fe, and the subsequent charge compensation is still dominated by Fe (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34c,d</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, the high-energy electrons of NFMMT are clustered around O, and a more pronounced anionic reaction will occur in the deep desodiation stage. The results suggest that the co-doping of Mg and Ti will enhance the oxygen reaction in NFMMT while eliminating other detrimental oxygen activities such as oxygen gas release and surface reactions of radical oxygen, which is consistent with the mRIXS results.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310659-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/144bf8bc-61f3-499e-8fcd-df654c28fbd2/adma202310659-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/144bf8bc-61f3-499e-8fcd-df654c28fbd2/adma202310659-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a8e7b3e6-2617-447f-81aa-4e44c1ed4a0a/adma202310659-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/144bf8bc-61f3-499e-8fcd-df654c28fbd2/adma202310659-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310659-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310659" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The model of NFM for the first principles calculations. b) Density of state and c) schematic illustrating the energy versus density of states in NFM. d) Model of NFMMT for the first principles calculations. e) Density of state and f) schematic illustrating the energy versus density of states in NFMMT. g) PDOS of differently coordinated O in NFMMT. h) Highest occupied state charge density for NFMMT (−1–0&nbsp;eV energy range).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>As discussed above, the co-doping of Mg and Ti can also lead to a more stable structure, which could be ascribed to the Ti-induced weakening of Jahn–Teller effect and Mn dissolution, and also the strong Mg─O bonds and Mg migration. To further understand the effect of Mg/Ti co-doping on the structure stability, we have examined the formation energies of typical O vacancies in the two materials. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35</a> (Supporting Information), the formation energies of two typical kinds of O vacancies in NFMMT are larger than that of NFM. Clearly, co-doping makes the lattice O more difficult to escape by forming O vacancy, thereby stabilizing the TMs dissolution and the layered framework.</p>
            
            <p>Oxygen ARR offers high energy to cathode materials, however, often suffers from fast decay upon cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0014_R_d132323700e1770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0024_R_d132323700e1770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Mater.">24</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0026_R_d132323700e1770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Joule">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is thus important to evaluate the stability of ARR in the two materials. Figure <a href="#adma202310659-fig-0007">7</a>&nbsp;shows the RIXS results of the fully charged states of the two materials in the first, second, and 50th cycles. The individual RIXS cuts at 531&nbsp;eV excitation energy display that, compared with that of NFM, the stronger ARR sustains a much more stable cycling in NFMMT. The inset mRIXS maps show the same result. For NFM, the ARR feature could still be observed at the second charged state, but no obvious feature could be seen at the 50th charged state, indicating ARR decays within tens of cycles in the NFM system. By contrast, for NFMMT, the ARR feature located at 523.7&nbsp;eV emission energy can be still clearly seen at the second and 50th charged states. This is also consistent with the electrochemical performance of the NFMMT electrode which displays both higher capacity and better cycling stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0002">2</a>).</p>
            
            <p>ARR quantification was also conducted based on our previously established method. The variations of the fingerprint feature intensity in O-K mRIXS were carefully quantified upon cycling, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310659-fig-0007">7</a>. Specifically, we first normalized all the spectra to the broad hybridization feature (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a>, Supporting Information), then calculated the intensity differences between the cycled electrodes and the pristine sample to obtain the oxidized oxygen peak variation upon cycling up to 50 cycles (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). As indicated by these quantifications, the ARRs of NFMMT and NFM show a decay of ≈34% (0.18 to 0.12) and ≈67% (0.09 to 0.03) after 50 cycles, respectively. The second cycle data show negligible changes based on such a spectroscopic estimation, indicating the decay does not take place suddenly as in Li-rich materials. Although this is only a rough estimation based on spectroscopic results, the big difference between NTMMT (66% retained after 50 cycles) and NFM (33% retained after 50 cycles) here implies that the ARR reversibility is indeed improved in NFMMT.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310659-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1adb0bd6-66fa-434f-9ceb-fa6b90b50b6a/adma202310659-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1adb0bd6-66fa-434f-9ceb-fa6b90b50b6a/adma202310659-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ecaa191b-92a6-4aa6-aeca-71786a50ab23/adma202310659-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1adb0bd6-66fa-434f-9ceb-fa6b90b50b6a/adma202310659-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310659-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310659" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Stability of Oxygen Anionic Redox Reaction. The RIXS cut peaks of NFM (left) and NFMMT (right) at the first, second, and 50th charged states. The inset images show the corresponding mRIXS results.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>We note that, at this time, there is no technique that could provide absolute quantification of the ARR charge transfer number, so we could only deduct the relative changes of the oxidized oxygen feature instead of the absolute value of the redox capacity. Nevertheless, combined with the TM analysis above, we can deliver an overall picture. Another conclusion through the quantification here is that NFMMT offers both stronger reactions and better reversibility of ARR than those of NFM. Compared with NFM, the improved capacity/voltage retention of NFMMT can be ascribed to the improved stabilities of the mixed and correlated CRR and ARR activities. In addition, the doped Mg and Ti may also play a role for the stabilization of the ARR process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0023_R_d132323700e1817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Chem.">23</a>, <a href="#adma202310659-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0026_R_d132323700e1817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Joule">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310659-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310659-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, this work designed a high-capacity and earth-abundant layered oxide cathode for advanced NIBs. The stable cathode material NFMMT&nbsp;(P2 Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>), shows a high capacity of &gt;200&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and good cycling stability even when charging/discharging between 1.5–4.5&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Compared with the pristine NFM&nbsp;(Na<sub>0.67</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) cathode, NFMMT significantly enhanced electrochemical, structural, and thermal stabilities, as demonstrated by the simpler electrochemical phase transition and less phase change of the fully charged NFMMT during heating, respectively. More importantly, NFMMT displays stronger ARR activities than NFM upon cycling, surprisingly, with clear improvements on stability. The stabilized ARR activities in NFMMT demonstrate that oxygen redox activities can be enhanced and stabilized synchronously. Our present findings provide useful insights into the optimization of dopants, for instance, multi-cations or high-entropy dopants, to improve the cycling stability of Fe/Mn-based layered oxide cathodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310659-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310659-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202310659-sec-0120-title"> Material Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>P2-NFM and P2-MFMMT were synthesized by a traditional solid-state reaction. For NFM, stoichiometric amounts of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.), MnO<sub>2</sub> (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.), and FeO (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.) were thoroughly mixed by ball milling overnight at 300&nbsp;rpm (ZrO<sub>2</sub> balls with a ball-to-powder ratio of 8:1) using a FRITSCH P7 8000D DUAL MIXER/MILL. The obtained mixture was dried at 100&nbsp;°C, calcined at 700&nbsp;°C for 5&nbsp;h, and then cooled in air (muffle furnace). After that, the mixture was pressed into pellets (300&nbsp;mg for each, prepared at 10&nbsp;MPa), which were calcined at 950&nbsp;°C in O<sub>2</sub> for 15&nbsp;h (tube furnace). The ramp rate was 5&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>. After cooling to room temperature, the obtained NFM was collected in an argon glove box. P2-NFMMT was synthesized under the same conditions with stoichiometric amounts of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.), MnO<sub>2</sub> (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.), FeO (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.), TiO<sub>2</sub> (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.), and MgO (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.). P2-NFMM and P2-NFMT were synthesized under the same conditions as that of P2-NFMMT except without TiO<sub>2</sub> or MgO, respectively.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202310659-sec-0130-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>Cathode active materials (NFM, NFMM, NFMT, or NFMMT), carbon black (MREDA), and polyvinylidenefluoride (PVDF, SOLVAY SOLEF PVDF 5130) with a weight ratio of 75:15:10 were used to prepare the electrode. PVDF was first added into <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidone (NMP) to prepare a uniform suspension. Then, the cathode materials and carbon black were added into the suspension, which were further mixed by a mixing machine. The slurry was then coated on Al foil with an areal density of 3.68–4.94&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and dried at 80&nbsp;°C in a vacuum oven for 12&nbsp;h. The obtained electrodes (12&nbsp;mm in diameter) were assembled in CR2032 coin-type cells under a pressure of 50&nbsp;MPa in an argon-filled glove box, using ≈150&nbsp;µL NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte (1&nbsp;mol&nbsp;L<sup>−1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate (PC) (Tomiyama Pure Chemical Industries, the same below), with 2&nbsp;vol.% fluorinated ethylene carbonate additive). The water content of the non-aqueous electrolyte solution was 20–40&nbsp;ppm. The Na metal (MACKLIN, AR, ≥99.5%) counter electrode was cut by knife in the Ar-filled glove box to remove the oxide layer and adjust the content, then the clean Na metal was pressed into disc with a diameter of 12&nbsp;mm and a thickness of ≈500&nbsp;µm. The Whatman GF/A glass fiber was used as the separator, which has a thickness of 260&nbsp;µm, a pore size of ≈1.6&nbsp;µm, and a diameter of 16&nbsp;mm. Electrochemical performance was tested by LAND CT2001A battery test system (Wuhan, PR China) in environmental chambers at a constant temperature of 25&nbsp;±&nbsp;1&nbsp;°C.</p>
                  
                  <p>CR2032 coin-type cells (MTI) with 2&nbsp;mm holes were used for in situ XRD measurement. In order to avoid exposure of cathode material to air, holes were sealed with plastic Kapton tape (Sigma-Aldrich), which had no XRD peak. Other steps were the same as the conventional coin cell assembling process, which were carried out at 50&nbsp;MPa in an argon-filled glove box.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202310659-sec-0140-title"> Material Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>To test the air stability, 1.5&nbsp;g NFM and 1.5&nbsp;g NFMMT powder materials were taken out from the glove box and exposed to air for a month, and then characterized by XRD (Bruker, advanced D8). The step was 0.02° and the rate was two seconds per step.</p>
                  
                  <p>For the ICP test, both NFM and NFMMT powder materials with a weight of 10&nbsp;mg were dissolved in 20&nbsp;mL chloroazotic acid, which was prepared by HCl (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.) and HNO<sub>3</sub> (Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd.). Then, 1&nbsp;mL of each solution was diluted by DI water to 100&nbsp;mL for the ICP (OPTIMA7000DV) test.</p>
                  
                  <p>XPS was used to identify the average valences of Mg and Ti after the activation of NFMMT in NIBs, which was performed by XPS (PHI Quantera II) with a monochromatic Al Kα X-ray source.</p>
                  
                  <p>In situ DEMS was carried out to study the different gases generated during cell cycling. The equipment consisted of a quadrupole mass spectrometer (Thermo Fischer) equipped with a turbomolecular pump and mass-flow controllers (Bronkhorst). The NIBs with gas inlet and outlet ports for the in situ DEMS test were assembled in a mold with either NFM or NFMMT (10&nbsp;mg) and 0.3&nbsp;mL NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte. The NIBs were tested by an electrochemical workstation (Chenhua, CHI1400) at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for three cycles to synchronously proceed the in situ DEMS analysis.</p>
                  
                  <p>Ex situ and in situ XRD patterns were collected at Sector 11-ID-C of the Advanced Photon Source (APS) in Argonne National Laboratory. The X-ray preset wavelength was 0.117418&nbsp;Å. The data was exchanged by Fit 2D software with 4000 points from 1° to 8° using CeO<sub>2</sub> as a standard reference material. Rietveld refinement was accomplished using the commercialized Topas software. High-temperature in situ XRD for both NFM and NFMMT were collected at Sector 11-ID-C of APS, with a ramp rate of 5&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> from 30 to 400&nbsp;°C. Both materials were assembled in CR2032 coin-type cells under 50&nbsp;MPa in an argon-filled glove box, using 150&nbsp;µL NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte. Then the cells were charged to 4.5&nbsp;V and maintained at the constant voltage for 20&nbsp;h. After that, the charged cells were disassembled in an Ar glove box. The cathode materials were washed with dimethyl carbonate (DMC) to remove the remaining electrolyte and then collected from the current collector. Subsequently, the cathode materials were transferred to a sealed crucible for high-temperature XRD test, in which the crucible was sealed by plastic Kapton tape.</p>
                  
                  <p>The atomic-resolution imaging and EDX mapping of cathode materials were performed on a double aberration-corrected JEOL-ARM200CF microscope with a cold field emission gun operating at 200 KV. The convergent semi-angle of 23&nbsp;mrad was adopted for high-angle annular dark field scanning transmission electron microscopy imaging (HAADF-STEM) and annular bright field STEM imaging (ABF-STEM) with the corresponding collection semi-angle of 90–370 and 11–23&nbsp;mrad, respectively. To improve the signal-to-noise ratio, atomically resolved STEM images were subjected to the average background subtraction filtering implemented in DigitalMicrograph software.</p>
                  
                  <p>XANES experiments on the Mn K-edge and Fe K-edge of both NFM and NFMMT were carried out in the transmission mode at Beamline 20-BM of the APS. O K-edge mRIXS was measured in the high-efficiency iRIXS end station at Beamline 8.0.1 of Advanced Light Source, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory. The energy resolution of the incident (excitation) X-ray beam was ≈0.2&nbsp;eV. RIXS cut at 531&nbsp;eV excitation energy was extracted from the corresponding mRIXS. All the samples were prepared in the argon-filled glove box. To avoid any air exposure, samples were sealed in a homemade suitcase<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310659-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310659-bib-0066_R_d132323700e1958" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - J. Electron Spectrosc. Relat. Phenom.">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and transferred into the experimental vacuum chamber.</p>
                  
                  <p>The <sup>57</sup>Fe Mössbauer spectroscopy characterization of NFMMT was carried out on an SEE Co W304 Mössbauer spectrometer, using a <sup>57</sup>Co/Rh source in transmission geometry. The data were fitted with the MossWinn 4.0 software.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310659-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202310659-sec-0150-title"> DFT Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed using the pseudopotentials based on projector augmented wave and the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerh implemented in the Vienna ab initio package. The strong correlation effect of Mn, Fe, and Ti was modified by DFT with Hubbard U correction. The Brillouin zone was adopted with a 3&nbsp;×&nbsp;2&nbsp;×&nbsp;2&nbsp;Γ-centered k-mesh, and the plane-wave basis was cut off by 520&nbsp;eV. All structures were relaxed, and the total energy converged was set to be 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV. For a more accurate band gap, the U values were set to Ti&nbsp;=&nbsp;2.5, Fe&nbsp;=&nbsp;4.9, and Mn&nbsp;=&nbsp;4.9&nbsp;eV. O-vacancy formation energy (∆<i>E</i>) is obtained by the energy difference between the O-deficient model and the pristine model (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202310659-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>)), where <i>x</i> is the amount of O removed from the lattice:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202310659-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/068f3006-8585-4f41-a58b-1f6cc4dd9636/adma202310659-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202310659:adma202310659-math-0006" display="block" location="graphic/adma202310659-math-0006.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                    <mi>E</mi>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>E</mi>
                                          <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi>O</mi>
                                                <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                                <mo>−</mo>
                                                <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                                <mi>d</mi>
                                                <mi>e</mi>
                                                <mi>f</mi>
                                                <mi>i</mi>
                                                <mi>c</mi>
                                                <mi>i</mi>
                                                <mi>e</mi>
                                                <mi>n</mi>
                                                <mi>t</mi>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </mfenced>
                                          <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                          <mo>+</mo>
                                          <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                          <mn>0.5</mn>
                                          <mi>x</mi>
                                          <mi>E</mi>
                                          <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>O</mi>
                                                <mn>2</mn>
                                             </msub>
                                          </mfenced>
                                          <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                          <mo>−</mo>
                                          <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                          <mi>E</mi>
                                          <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi>p</mi>
                                                <mi>r</mi>
                                                <mi>i</mi>
                                                <mi>s</mi>
                                                <mi>t</mi>
                                                <mi>i</mi>
                                                <mi>n</mi>
                                                <mi>e</mi>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </mfenced>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mn>0.5</mn>
                                          <mi>x</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\Delta E\ \ = \ \frac{{E\left( {O\ - \ deficient} \right)\ + \ 0.5xE\left( {{{O}_2}} \right)\ - \ E\left( {pristine} \right)}}{{0.5x}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
                  </div>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
